<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Musa balbisiana genome reveals subgenome evolution and functional divergence"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2019 5:8"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-07-15"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Banana cultivars (Musa ssp.) are diploid, triploid and tetraploid hybrids derived from Musa acuminata and Musa balbisiana. We presented a high-quality draft genome assembly of M. balbisiana with 430&#8201;Mb (87%) assembled into 11&#8201;chromosomes. We identified that the recent divergence of M. acuminata (A-genome) and M. balbisiana (B-genome) occurred after lineage-specific whole-genome duplication, and that the B-genome may be more sensitive to the fractionation process compared to the A-genome. Homoeologous exchanges occurred frequently between A- and B-subgenomes in allopolyploids. Genomic variation within progenitors resulted in functional divergence of subgenomes. Global homoeologue expression dominance occurred between subgenomes of the allotriploid. Gene families related to ethylene biosynthesis and starch metabolism exhibited significant expansion at the pathway level and wide homoeologue expression dominance in the B-subgenome of the allotriploid. The independent origin of 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase (ACO) homoeologue gene pairs and tandem duplication-driven expansion of ACO genes in the B-subgenome contributed to rapid and major ethylene production post-harvest in allotriploid banana fruits. The findings of this study provide greater context for understanding fruit biology, and aid the development of tools for breeding optimal banana cultivars. A high-quality draft genome assembly of Musa balbisiana (the banana B-genome) is reported. Comparative genomic and gene functional analyses provide insights into the subgenome evolution and functional divergence between the A- and B-subgenomes."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Banana cultivars (<i>Musa</i> ssp.) are diploid, triploid and tetraploid hybrids derived from <i>Musa acuminata</i> and <i>Musa balbisiana</i>. We presented a high-quality draft genome assembly of <i>M. balbisiana</i> with 430 Mb (87%) assembled into 11 chromosomes. We identified that the recent divergence of <i>M. acuminata</i> (A-genome) and <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B-genome) occurred after lineage-specific whole-genome duplication, and that the B-genome may be more sensitive to the fractionation process compared to the A-genome. Homoeologous exchanges occurred frequently between A- and B-subgenomes in allopolyploids. Genomic variation within progenitors resulted in functional divergence of subgenomes. Global homoeologue expression dominance occurred between subgenomes of the allotriploid. Gene families related to ethylene biosynthesis and starch metabolism exhibited significant expansion at the pathway level and wide homoeologue expression dominance in the B-subgenome of the allotriploid. The independent origin of 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase (ACO) homoeologue gene pairs and tandem duplication-driven expansion of <i>ACO</i> genes in the B-subgenome contributed to rapid and major ethylene production post-harvest in allotriploid banana fruits. The findings of this study provide greater context for understanding fruit biology, and aid the development of tools for breeding optimal banana cultivars.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Bananas (<i>Musa</i> ssp.) are large herbaceous plants that are perennial but monocarpic. They originated in Southeast Asia and the Western Pacific and were one of the first crops to be domesticated, about 7,000 years ago, in Southeast Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64575e971">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davey, M. W. et al. A draft Musa balbisiana genome sequence for molecular genetics in polyploid, inter-and intra-specific Musa hybrids. BMC Genom. 14, 683 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64575e974">2</a></sup>. Bananas are widely distributed throughout the tropics and subtropics, where they are a staple food and fruit for millions of people<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davey, M. W. et al. A draft Musa balbisiana genome sequence for molecular genetics in polyploid, inter-and intra-specific Musa hybrids. BMC Genom. 14, 683 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64575e978">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Langhe, E. et al. Why bananas matter: an introduction to the history of banana domestication. Ethnobot. Res. Appl. 7, 165–177 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64575e981">3</a></sup>. Moreover, bananas are one of the major export commodities of several developing countries and represent the largest international trade in fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Langhe, E. et al. Why bananas matter: an introduction to the history of banana domestication. Ethnobot. Res. Appl. 7, 165–177 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64575e985">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Paul, J. Y. et al. Golden bananas in the field: elevated fruit pro‐vitamin A from the expression of a single banana transgene. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 520–532 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d64575e988">4</a></sup>. Thus, bananas are an essential food resource and have important socioeconomic and ecological roles.</p><p>The genus <i>Musa</i> belongs to the monocotyledon Musaceae family along with the genus <i>Ensete</i>. Its wild species have traditionally been subdivided into four sections: <i>Eumusa</i> (<i>x</i> = 11; <i>x</i> represents the number of chromosomes), <i>Rhodochlamys</i> (<i>x</i> = 11), <i>Australimusa</i> (<i>x</i> = 10) and <i>Callimusa</i> (<i>x</i> = 9 or 10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cheesman, E. E. Classification of the bananas. Kew Bull. 2, 97–117 (1947)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1030">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Simmonds, N. W. &amp; Shepherd, K. The taxonomy and origins of the cultivated bananas. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 55, 302–312 (1955)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1030_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Daniells, J. et al. Diversity in the Genus Musa (INIBAP, 2001)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1033">7</a></sup>, and refined recently to two sections in which the <i>Rhodochlamys</i> and <i>Australimusa</i> were merged into the <i>Eumusa</i> and <i>Callimusa</i>, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Häkkinen, M. Reappraisal of sectional taxonomy in Musa (Musaceae). Taxon 62, 809–813 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1049">8</a></sup>. Most edible bananas belong to the <i>Eumusa</i> (or <i>Musa</i>) section, and are categorized into the dessert or cooking group based on their usage. Furthermore, bananas of this section are distinguished based on their genetic background as <i>Musa acuminata</i> (A-genome, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 22; <i>n</i> represents the haploid chromosome number), <i>Musa balbisiana</i> (B-genome, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 22), <i>Musa schizocarpa</i> (S-genome, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 22) and <i>Australimusa</i> species (T-genome, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 20)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davey, M. W. et al. A draft Musa balbisiana genome sequence for molecular genetics in polyploid, inter-and intra-specific Musa hybrids. BMC Genom. 14, 683 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1101">2</a></sup>. The majority of edible cultivated bananas originated from intraspecific or interspecific hybridization between wild diploid <i>M. acuminata</i> (A-genome) and <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B-genome) species. Combinations of these A- and B-genomes have resulted in various genotypes of cultivated edible bananas, including diploid (AA, BB and AB), triploid (AAA, AAB and ABB) and tetraploid (AAAB, AABB, ABBB) variants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Simmonds, N. W. &amp; Shepherd, K. The taxonomy and origins of the cultivated bananas. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 55, 302–312 (1955)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1112">6</a></sup>. The triploid genotype variants constitute the predominant cultivated varieties that are planted worldwide.</p><p>Genome sequencing of the A-genome banana has provided insights into the evolution of monocotyledonous plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1119">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Martin, G. et al. Improvement of the banana ‘Musa acuminata’ reference sequence using NGS data and semi-automated bioinformatics methods. BMC Genom. 17, 243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1122">9</a></sup>. Although the A-genome represents a crucial step in the genetic improvement of banana, the lack of a high-quality B-genome sequence greatly hinders germplasm characterization and the molecular breeding of banana. A draft B-genome has previously been reported, but exhibited low quality, based on assembly and annotation via mapping reads to the A-genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davey, M. W. et al. A draft Musa balbisiana genome sequence for molecular genetics in polyploid, inter-and intra-specific Musa hybrids. BMC Genom. 14, 683 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1126">2</a></sup>. Here, we sequenced the genome of the double haploid of the wild diploid genotype Pisang Klutuk Wulung (DH-PKW, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 22), belonging to the species <i>M. balbisiana</i> that contributed the B-subgenome to cultivated allotriploid bananas. We further compared the B- and A-genomes to investigate subgenome evolution, genetic diversity and the functional divergence of subgenomes in polyploid bananas. Our analyses provide insights into the evolution and regulation of fruit-ripening processes in bananas. In particular, the results highlight a significant contribution of the B-genome towards ethylene biosynthesis and starch metabolism during fruit ripening.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>To reduce heterozygosity, we used the DH-PKW genotype for our genome sequencing and assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Assani, A. et al. Production of haploids from anther culture of banana [Musa balbisiana (BB)]. Plant Cell Rep. 21, 511–516 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1151">10</a></sup>. A total of 58.99 gigabases (Gb) (113×) of PacBio single-molecule long reads and 86.34 Gb (166×) Illumina paired-end and mate-pair reads were used for assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">1</a>), producing 492.77 megabases (Mb) of scaffolds. The contig N50 and scaffold N50 of the final assembly were 1.83 and 5.05 Mb, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">2</a>). <i>K</i>-mer analysis suggested that the draft assembly covers approximately 95% of the genome size of DH-PKW (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">1</a>). We further evaluated the completeness of the scaffold assembly using the BUSCO (v.3) plants datasets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1168">11</a></sup>. Precise exon placement of 91.3% of the total 1,440 single-copy orthologue groups in the embryophyta dataset was identified in the B-genome assembly. To anchor the scaffolds to chromosomes, we constructed high-throughput chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) libraries of DH-PKW, generating 72 Gb (138×) Hi-C pair-end reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">1</a>). Duplicate removal, sorting and quality assessment were performed with HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1175">12</a></sup>, and uniquely mapped valid reads were used for Hi-C scaffolding by LACHESIS software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1179">13</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">2</a>). As a result, 430 Mb (87.27%) of the assembly and 94.0% of the genes were placed on 11 chromosome groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">3</a>). The 11 pseudo-molecules were named in accordance with the <i>M. acuminata</i> (A-genome) reference sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1196">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Martin, G. et al. Improvement of the banana ‘Musa acuminata’ reference sequence using NGS data and semi-automated bioinformatics methods. BMC Genom. 17, 243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1199">9</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Characterization of M. balbisiana (B-genome) and M. acuminata (A-genome) chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Characterization of <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B-genome) and <i>M. acuminata</i> (A-genome) chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="685" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Elements are arranged in the following scheme (from outer to inner). (1) Distribution of Gypsy elements (non-overlapping, window size, 50 kb); (2) distribution of Copia elements (non-overlapping, window size, 50 kb); (3) distribution of orthologous gene pairs between two genomes (non-overlapping, window size, 50 kb); (4) gene density (non-overlapping, window size, 50 kb); (5) syntenic relationships between A- and B-genomes. The connecting blue lines represent alignment blocks, red lines represent inversions, green lines represent translocations and grey lines show small blocks with&lt;30 gene pairs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>About 55.75% of the B-genome assembly was composed of repetitive sequences, which is higher than the 41.85% of the A-genome assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">4</a>). This may be due to the spanning of repetitive regions by long reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Li, C. et al. Genome sequencing and assembly by long reads in plants. Genes 9, 6 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1235">14</a></sup>. Long terminal-repeat (LTR) retrotransposons represented the most abundant fraction of transposable elements in the A- and B-genomes, among which the families Gypsy and Copia accounted for 12.88 and 28.04% of the B-genome, respectively. DNA transposons comprised 2.12% of the B-genome and 2.03% of the A-genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">4</a>). LTR retrotransposons tended to accumulate near the centromeric and pericentromeric regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig1">1</a>). Active insertions of LTR retrotransposons occurred more recently in the B-genome (peak at 0–0.5 million years ago (MYA)) relative to the A-genome (peak at 1.5–2.0 MYA) after their divergence (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">3</a>). Both genomes experienced a wave of LTR retrotransposon amplification but differed in insertion histories.</p><p>We annotated the genome using the MAKER pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. Genome annotation and curation using MAKER and MAKER‐P. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 48, 4–11 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1251">15</a></sup> incorporating ab initio predictions, homologous proteins and transcriptome data from six samples, resulting in 35,148 protein-coding genes in the B-genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">5</a>). Of these, 33,137 (94.27%) were located on the 11 pseudo-chromosomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">3</a>). Overall, 86% of the genes transcribed in our RNA sequence (RNA-Seq) analysis. Additionally, we identified 3,329 transcription factors among 88 families in the B-genome using the iTAK programme<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zheng, Y. et al. iTAK: a program for genome-wide prediction and classification of plant transcription factors, transcriptional regulators, and protein kinases. Mol. Plant 9, 1667–1670 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1261">16</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">The B-genome is more sensitive to fractionation than the A-genome</h3><p>Compared to other monocots, the <i>Musa</i> lineage exhibits a relatively slower evolutionary rate as demonstrated previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Monocot plastid phylogenomics, timeline, net rates of species diversification, the power of multi-gene analyses, and a functional model for the origin of monocots. Am. J. Bot. 105, 1888–1910 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1279">17</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">4</a>). Phylogenetic analyses based on 519 single-copy orthologous genes indicated a very recent divergence time of about 5.4 MYA for the A- and B-genomes, with their common ancestor having diverged from Poaceae ~134 MYA (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">5</a>). This estimate is consistent with a divergence time of 4.6 MYA between the A- and B-genomes, which was estimated based on 17 bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) clones that contained 23.5 Kb of coding sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lescot, M. et al. Insights into the Musa genome: syntenic relationships to rice and between Musa species. BMC Genomics 9, 58 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1289">18</a></sup>. Our estimate is more recent than the 20.9 MYA (estimated by three genes and one internal transcribed spacer region) or the 27.9 MYA (estimated by 19F genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Christelova, P. et al. A multi gene sequence-based phylogeny of the Musaceae (banana) family. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 103 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1294">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Janssens, S. B. et al. Evolutionary dynamics and biogeography of Musaceae reveal a correlation between the diversification of the banana family and the geological and climatic history of Southeast Asia. New Phytol. 210, 1453–1465 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1297">20</a></sup>. However, the increased sampling of informative characters in our genome-wide study for phylogenetic analyses should contribute to a more accurate divergence time estimation.</p><p>Three rounds of whole-genome duplication (WGD) events (α-, β- and γ-WGD) have occurred in the <i>Musa</i> lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1307">1</a></sup>, which was validated by our four-fold synonymous third-codon transversion (4dTv) analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">6</a>). WGD is frequently, and almost always, followed by diploidization and fractionation, which includes chromosome rearrangement, gene loss and biased retention<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mandáková, T. et al. How diploidization turned a tetraploid into a pseudotriploid. Am. J. Bot. 103, 1187–1196 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1314">21</a></sup>. After diploidization and divergence, A- and B-genomes start to evolve independently. To investigate the evolutionary differences between the A- and B-genomes after divergence, we performed three types of comparative analysis: (1) assessment of gene family expansion/contraction between A- and B-genomes by comparison to 14 other plant genomes; (2) comparison of structural variation in the A- and B-genomes by synteny analysis; and (3) comparison of synteny between the ancestral syntenic block and the A/B-genomes.</p><p>We analysed the gene family clustering and expansion/contraction of banana genomes of <i>M. acuminat</i><i>a</i> (A-genome) and <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B-genome), compared to 14 other plant genomes using OrthoMCL and CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1329">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="De Bie, T. et al. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1332">23</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">7</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">7</a>). We found 9,038 gene families that were conserved in <i>M. balbisiana</i>, <i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. In contrast, 348 and 639 gene families were specific to the A- and B-genome, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">8</a>). After their divergence, 1,761 gene families were expanded and 203 were contracted in the A-genome, while 392 gene families were expanded and 1,008 contracted in the B-genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">9</a>). Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) pathway enrichment analysis of the significantly expanded gene families (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) in the B-genome suggested that it was enriched in the photosynthesis and biosynthesis of secondary metabolite pathways, including those associated with the metabolism of inositol, starch and sucrose, linoleic acid and arachidonic acid (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">10</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">10</a>). Plants produce a high diversity of secondary metabolites with prominent functions in defence against a variety of herbivores and pathogens, in addition to the mitigation of various types of abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bennett, R. N. &amp; Wallsgrove, R. M. Secondary metabolites in plant defence mechanisms. New Phytol. 127, 617–633 (1994)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1381">24</a></sup>. Thus, these observations are consistent with the association of the B-genome with improved vigour and tolerance to both biotic and abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davey, M. W. et al. A draft Musa balbisiana genome sequence for molecular genetics in polyploid, inter-and intra-specific Musa hybrids. BMC Genom. 14, 683 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1385">2</a></sup>.</p><p>Synteny analysis indicated a high level of genomic colinearity and sequence similarity between the A- and B-genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">11</a>). We identified 72 large syntenic blocks between the A- and B-genomes, including 15 large blocks each containing over 900 gene pairs. These 72 syntenic blocks comprised 75.02% of A-genome space (containing 23% transposable elements) and 68.01% of B-genome space (containing 22% transposable elements) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">11</a>). We also identified two large translocations and two inversions between the A- and B-genome after their divergence. One large reciprocal translocation comprises 7.09 Mb on chromosome (chr)1 of the B-genome and 7.03 Mb on chr:3 of the A-genome, and one large inversion of 9.39 Mb on chr 5 of the B-genome and 8.83 Mb on chr 5 of the A-genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">11</a>). These translocations and inversions were also supported by the rearrangements based on genetic mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Baurens, F. C. et al. Recombination and large structural variations shape interspecific edible bananas genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 36, 97–111 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1404">25</a></sup>, and can serve to introduce novel genetic diversity into the A- and B-genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Saxena, R. K., Edwards, D. &amp; Varshney, R. K. Structural variations in plant genomes. Brief. Funct. Genom. 13, 296–307 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1409">26</a></sup>.</p><p>Previously, 12 <i>Musa</i> ancestral blocks were assembled that represented the ancestral genome before α/β-WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1420">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jourda, C. et al. Expansion of banana (Musa acuminata) gene families involved in ethylene biosynthesis and signalling after lineage specific whole genome duplications. New Phytol. 202, 986–1000 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1423">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Garsmeur, O. et al. Two evolutionarily distinct classes of paleopolyploidy. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 448–454 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1426">28</a></sup>. We identified 97 syntenic blocks resulting from α/β-WGD events in the B-genome by comparison to the 12 <i>Musa</i> ancestral blocks. These blocks contained 24,639 genes and represented 70.10% of all gene models involved in WGD-driven regions. We also identified 100 syntenic blocks that contained 26,780 genes (75.61%) in the A-genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">12</a>). Of these ancestral <i>Musa</i> α/β blocks, 56.89% (15,236) and 60.59% (14,930) were singletons in the A- and B-genome, respectively, suggesting that genome fractionation (gene loss) and diploidization processes had occurred extensively after WGD events in the <i>Musa</i> lineage (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">12</a>).</p><p>Taken together, our results indicated that the B-genome exhibited less expansion and more contraction of gene families, less syntenic coverage ratio and a higher singleton ratio in the ancestral blocks compared to the A-genome after divergence. Cycles of WGD followed by diploidization and fractionation have occurred across land plants, and are important in determining chromosome structure and gene content. Consequently, these processes have significantly contributed to the evolutionary success of plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mandáková, T. et al. How diploidization turned a tetraploid into a pseudotriploid. Am. J. Bot. 103, 1187–1196 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1449">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Dodsworth, S., Chase, M. W. &amp; Leitch, A. R. Is post‐polyploidization diploidization the key to the evolutionary success of angiosperms? Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 180, 1–5 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1452">29</a></sup>. The diploidization and fractionation processes involve a series of evolutionary events, including repetitive DNA loss, chromosome rearrangements and complex patterns of gene loss<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Dodsworth, S., Chase, M. W. &amp; Leitch, A. R. Is post‐polyploidization diploidization the key to the evolutionary success of angiosperms? Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 180, 1–5 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1456">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Langham, R. J. et al. Genomic duplication, fractionation and the origin of regulatory novelty. Genetics 166, 935–945 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1459">30</a></sup>. The above evidence supports the hypothesis that the B-genome was more sensitive to fractionation than the A-genome after their divergence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genetic diversity in polyploid bananas and functional divergence of subgenomes</h3><p>Polyploid species usually exhibit vigorous growth, including high-quality production and high fitness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ni, Z. et al. Altered circadian rhythms regulate growth vigor in hybrids and allopolyploids. Nature 457, 327 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1471">31</a></sup>. Most banana cultivars are polyploid and exhibit various levels of ploidy and genomic background<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="de Jesus, O. N. et al. Genetic diversity and population structure of Musa accessions in ex situ conservation. BMC Plant Biol. 13, 41 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1475">32</a></sup>. The genetic classification of some bananas is discordant, as is the case for Pelipita. Previous studies have shown that its karyotype comprises eight A and 25 B chromosomes as opposed to the predicted 11 A and 22 B chromosome distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The interspecific genome structure of cultivated banana, Musa spp. revealed by genomic DNA in situ hybridization. Theo. Appl. Genet. 100, 177–183 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1479">33</a></sup>. Understanding the genetic diversity and genomic constitution of <i>Musa</i> accessions would inform genomic group classifications, in addition to conservation and breeding strategies. Therefore, we re-sequenced five triploid bananas and four diploid bananas to investigate their genetic diversity (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">13</a>).</p><p>Simultaneous alignment of re-sequencing data to the A- and B-subgenomes identified the uniquely mapped reads that were used to analyse coverage depth, variations calling and homoeologous exchanges on each chromosome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">14</a>). Homoeologous exchanges were characterized by read coverage that showed a chromosomal region with a duplicated copy from the corresponding homoeologous subgenome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chalhoub, Boulos et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1495">34</a></sup>. These analyses confirmed that genome constitutions for the banana accessions were, in most cases, consistent with previous genome group classifications based on morphological traits. We identified 48 segmental homoeologous exchanges in the accession FenJiao, including nine from the B- to the A-subgenome and 39 in the reverse direction (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">15</a>). We also found four segmental homoeologous exchanges from the B- to the A-subgenome in the accession Kamaramasenge, and replacement of chromosome 10 of the B-subgenome by the A-subgenome. (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">15</a>). For the accession Pelipita, chromosomes 2, 7 and 11 of the A-subgenome were replaced by the B-subgenome and there were 18 segmental homoeologous exchanges on chromosomes 6, 9 and 10 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">15</a>). This indicates the eight A and 25 B chromosome constitution of Pelipita, consistent with previous genomic in situ hybridization studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The interspecific genome structure of cultivated banana, Musa spp. revealed by genomic DNA in situ hybridization. Theo. Appl. Genet. 100, 177–183 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1518">33</a></sup>. This classification is further supported by phylogenetic analyses based on genotyping data (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">12</a>). A total of 18,475,661 single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), 1,425,391 small insertions and deletions and 220,452 structural variations were identified in the samples (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">18</a>). Analysis of gene and SNP density on the chromosomes indicated that SNPs were preferentially located on non-gene-rich regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">13</a>). There were ~2.5-fold SNPs on the A-genome of Pisang_Mas and Pisang_Kra compared to the B-genome of Balbisiana (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">16</a>). The nucleotide diversity (π,0.0059) of A-subgenomes was higher than that of the B-subgenomes (0.0031) in accessions Fenjiao, Pelipita and Kamaramasenge.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Coverage depth and genome structure summary for three allotriploid banana accessions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Coverage depth and genome structure summary for three allotriploid banana accessions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="337" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>–<b>c</b>, Chromosome coverage and structure for accessions FenJiao (genome group, ABB) (<b>a</b>), Kamaramasenge (genome group, AAB) (<b>b</b>) and Pelipita (genome group, ABB) (<b>c</b>) with 100 kb non-overlapping sliding windows. The upper red bar and lower blue bar represent coverage depth of the A- and B-subgenome, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Gene family expansion and contraction analysis of <i>M. acuminata</i> and <i>M. balbisiana</i> in comparison to other sequenced genomes indicated that there are 83 gene families significantly expanded in the A-genome (and conversely contracted in the B-genome). These families included plant–pathogen interactions, glycosphingolipid biosynthesis-ganglio series and glycosaminoglycan degradation pathways among others (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">19</a>). Conversely, 33 gene families were significantly expanded in the B-genome (and contracted in the A-genome). These families included those involved in photosynthesis, metabolic pathways and ribosome, among others (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">20</a>). This indicates that the A- and B-genomes may have functionally diverged at the genome level during their respective genome evolution.</p><p>To explore the transcription of allopolyploid subgenomes, we assessed the expression of homoeologue genes from the A- and B-subgenomes of the triploid FenJiao. Expression levels were measured within different tissues, at different stages of fruit development and ripening and in banana seedlings after abiotic stress treatments (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">21</a>). A total of 25,717 homoeologous gene pairs were identified between the A- and B-subgenomes based on the gene alignment method with cumulative identity percentage (CIP) ≥ 60% and cumulative alignment length percentage (CALP) ≥ 60% (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Salse, J. et al. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief. Bioinformatics 10, 619–630 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1593">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908–14913 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1596">36</a></sup>). Among the homoeologue gene pairs, 81.83% were further supported by syntenic analysis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">22</a>). Expression of all homoeologue gene pairs was assessed to determine the distribution of expression fold change of B/A in the triploid ‘FJ’. The log<sub>2</sub>(RPKM B/RPKM A) was 1.2/1, where RPKM is reads per kilobase million, which differed from the genomic constitution value of 2/1 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">14</a>). This result could be explained by dosage compensation, wherein the triploid expression levels are reduced to a diploid state<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ren, L. et al. Determination of dosage compensation and comparison of gene expression in a triploid hybrid fish. BMC Genom. 18, 38 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1609">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Guo, M., Davis, D. &amp; Birchler, J. A. Dosage effects on gene expression in a maize ploidy series. Genetics 142, 1349–1355 (1996)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1612">38</a></sup>. We further characterized 1,075 and 4,032 homoeologue gene pairs that showed expression dominance in the A- and B-subgenome, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">23</a>). KEGG enrichment analysis indicated that genes with expression dominance in the B-subgenome were associated with 2-oxocarboxylic acid metabolism and the arginine biosynthesis pathway (<i>q</i>-value &lt; 0.05) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">24</a>), whereas those showing expression dominance in the A-subgenome were not significantly enriched in KEGG pathways.</p><p>Non-synonymous/synonymous substitution (Ka/Ks) ratios were calculated for all homoeologue gene pairs between the A- and B-subgenomes. The Ka/Ks ratios of genes with expression dominance in the A-subgenome (median, 0.157) were slightly lower than those in the B-subgenome (median, 0.196) and non-dominant genes (median, 0.186) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><p>We then constructed a gene co-expression network for those genes with expression dominance using weighted gene co-expression network analysis (WGCNA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Langfelder, P. &amp; Horvath, S. WGCNA: an R package for weighted correlation network analysis. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 559 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1635">39</a></sup>. The results indicated that 87 and 295 genes with dominance expression interacted with 4,302 and 4,612 genes in the A- and B-subgenome, respectively. KEGG pathway enrichment analysis suggested that genes in the co-expression network of the A- and B-subgenomes were commonly associated with starch and sucrose metabolism (ko00500) and other metabolic pathways. In particular, ubiquinone and other terpenoid-quinone biosynthesis, photosynthesis–antenna proteins, carotenoid biosynthesis and other glycan degradation pathways were specially enriched in the A-subgenome (<i>q</i>-value &lt; 0.05) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">16</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">25</a>). In contrast, selenocompound metabolism and cyanoamino acid metabolism pathways were particularly enriched in the B-subgenome (<i>q</i>-value &lt; 0.05) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">17</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">26</a>). Overall, these results further support the hypothesis of functional divergence between the A- and B-genomes at the transcriptional level.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Expression dominance of homoeologue gene pairs in the ethylene biosynthesis pathway and expansion of the ACO family affect fruit ripening</h3><p>Ethylene plays a key role in the regulation of climacteric fruit ripening post-harvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kumar, R., Khurana, A. &amp; Sharma, A. K. Role of plant hormones and their interplay in development and ripening of fleshy fruits. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 4561–4575 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1666">40</a></sup>. The core enzymatic steps in the ethylene biosynthesis pathways are well characterized, and include S-adenosyl-<span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-methionine synthase (SAMS), 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid synthase (ACS) and ACO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Adams, D. O. &amp; Yang, S. F. Ethylene biosynthesis: identification of 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid as an intermediate in the conversion of methionine to ethylene. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 76, 170–174 (1979)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1673">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yang, S. F. &amp; Hoffman, N. E. Ethylene biosynthesis and its regulation in higher plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. 35, 155–189 (1984)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1676">42</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3a</a>). However, the expansion and expression dominance of these homoeologue gene pairs during polyploid fruit ripening remains largely unknown.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogeny and expression patterns of ethylene biosynthesis genes between M. acuminata (A-genome) and M. balbisiana (B-genome)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Phylogeny and expression patterns of ethylene biosynthesis genes between <i>M. acuminata</i> (A-genome) and <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B-genome).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="935" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Overview of the ethylene biosynthesis pathway. <b>b</b>, Expression patterns of SAMS, ACS and ACO family genes in the root and leaf, and at different stages of fruit development and ripening in BX, the A-subgenome of FJ and the B-subgenome of FJ. Genes aligned horizontally in the heat map indicate homoeologue gene pairs between the A- and B-genomes. White boxes with diagonals indicate the lack of homoeologue gene pairs between the A- and B-subgenomes. Asterisks indicate expression dominance of homoeologue gene pairs between the A- and B-subgenomes of FJ. <b>c</b>,<b>d</b>, Synteny analysis of ACS (<b>c</b>) and ACO (<b>d</b>) families between the A- and B-genomes. Red lines indicate paralogous gene pairs resulting from WGD, blue lines indicate homoeologous gene pairs, purple lines indicate tandem duplication, light blue strips indicate aligned syntenic blocks, light green strip indicates translocation block and light red strips indicate inversion blocks.The blocks in outer ring represent location and length of genes; blue blocks represent genes from A-genome and orange blocks represent genes from B-genome. <b>e</b>, Phylogenetic analysis of ACO family genes among nine species: <i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>M. balbisiana</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, <i>Asparagus officinalis</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i>. <b>f</b>, Ethylene production at different stages of fruit development and ripening in BX and FJ. Error bars show standard error of the mean from three independent experiments (<i>n</i> = 3).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified 12 SAMS, 11 ACS and 11 ACO genes from the A-genome and 10 SAMS, 11 ACS and 18 ACO genes in the B-genome, which represents a significant expansion compared to the seven other sequenced plant species among the monocots and eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1768">22</a></sup> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">27</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">28</a>). We further characterized 28 homoeologue gene pairs from the A- and B-genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">29</a>). These gene pairs displayed similar expression profiles in the BaXiJiao (<i>Musa</i> AAA group, cv. Cavendish, BX), the A-subgenome of FenJiao (<i>Musa</i> ABB group, cv Pisang Awak, FJ) and the B-subgenome of FJ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">30</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">32</a>). Interestingly, eight gene pairs exhibited homoeologue expression dominance in the B-subgenome and five gene pairs were dominantly expressed in the A-subgenome of FJ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">33</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">34</a>). The dominant expression of these genes in various tissues may be related to the fundamental role of ethylene biosynthesis.</p><p>Both ACS and ACO have previously been demonstrated as limiting enzymes within ethylene biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Adams, D. O. &amp; Yang, S. F. Ethylene biosynthesis: identification of 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid as an intermediate in the conversion of methionine to ethylene. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 76, 170–174 (1979)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1810">41</a></sup>. The expression abundance of <i>MA-ACS1</i> (AB021906) and <i>MA-ACO1</i> (X91076) is consistent with ethylene production during the post-harvest banana-ripening period<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Liu, X. et al. Characterization of ethylene biosynthesis associated with ripening in banana fruit. Plant Physiol. 121, 1257–1265 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1820">43</a></sup>. Of the ten <i>ACS</i> gene pairs, <i>MaACS7</i>/<i>MbACS7</i>, which is a homologue of <i>MA-ACS1</i>, exhibited high expression levels during fruit ripening and was dominantly expressed in the B-genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3b</a>). <i>MbACS6</i> and <i>MbACS7</i> are paralogous in a large syntenic block (the block contains 19 gene pairs), and maintain synteny and close evolutionary relationships to <i>MaACS6</i> and <i>MaACS7</i>, respectively, suggesting that these genes duplicated from WGD (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">18</a>). Of the nine <i>ACO</i> gene pairs, three (<i>MaACO2</i>/<i>MbACO6</i>, <i>MaACO3</i>/<i>MbACO7</i> and <i>MaACO8/MbACO13</i>) exhibited high expression levels during fruit ripening and were dominantly expressed in the B-subgenome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3b</a>). <i>MaACO2</i>/<i>MbACO6</i> and <i>MaACO3</i>/<i>MbACO7</i> are in the syntenic block of chr:5 and chr:6 between the A- and B-genome, respectively, and belong to the same phylogenetic clade (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3d,e</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">35</a>). These results indicated that <i>MaACO2</i>/<i>MbACO6</i> and <i>MaACO3</i>/<i>MbACO7</i> originated independently and developed crucial functions during fruit ripening.</p><p>We also observed high expression levels (log<sub>2</sub>RPKM &gt; 4 in at least one stage of fruit ripening in the B-genome) of homoeologue gene pairs, probably related to fruit softening (pectin methylesterases, galactosidases, expansions and pectatelyase)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Iqbal, N. et al. Ethylene role in plant growth, development and senescence: interaction with other phytohormones. Front. Plant. Sci. 8, 475 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1918">44</a></sup>, cell wall modification (xyloglucan endotransglucosylase/hydrolases, fasciclin-like arabinogalactan proteins and β-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-xylosidase)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1925">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Teh, B. T. et al. The draft genome of tropical fruit durian (Durio zibethinus). Nat. Genet. 49, 1633 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1928">46</a></sup> and aroma production (alcohol dehydrogenases)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kumar, R., Khurana, A. &amp; Sharma, A. K. Role of plant hormones and their interplay in development and ripening of fleshy fruits. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 4561–4575 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1932">40</a></sup>. These genes are closely involved in fruit ripening and are regulated by ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Iqbal, N. et al. Ethylene role in plant growth, development and senescence: interaction with other phytohormones. Front. Plant. Sci. 8, 475 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1937">44</a></sup>. Almost all of these gene pairs showed expression dominance in the B-subgenome of FJ during fruit ripening, which is the same as the dominance expression of gene pairs related to ethylene biosynthesis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">19</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">36</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">37</a>). This co-dominance of homoeologue gene pairs in the B-subgenome further supports the significant contribution of the B-genome to ethylene biosynthesis and fruit ripening.</p><p>Gene duplication is a major mechanism that generates new genetic diversity as a basis for evolutionary innovation in eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151–1155 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d64575e1954">47</a></sup>. Compared to the 11 <i>ACO</i> genes within the A-genome, the <i>ACO</i> genes in the B-genome expanded significantly to 18 members. The expansion of <i>ACO</i> genes, including <i>MbACO2</i>, <i>-3</i>, <i>-4</i>, <i>-5</i> in chr 3, <i>MbACO8</i>, <i>-9</i>, <i>-11</i> in chr 6 and <i>MbACO16</i>, <i>-18</i> in scaffolds, was driven by tandem duplications in the B-genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3b,d</a>). Of the <i>ACO</i> genes that were expanded in the B-genome, <i>MbACO2</i> and <i>MbACO3</i> showed strong expression levels during the fruit-ripening stages with log<sub>2</sub>RPKM &gt; 11 at 6 days post-harvest (DPH) in FJ, which is coincident with the ethylene climacteric period (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3b,f</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">38</a>). In addition, <i>MbACO8</i>, <i>-9</i>, <i>-11</i>, <i>-16</i>, <i>-18</i> exhibited high expression levels in roots and fruits at 0 days after flowering (DAF) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">38</a>). These genes belonged to the same cluster and their expression patterns were highly concordant with their duplication (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig3">3d,e</a>), suggesting that the expansion and evolution of <i>ACO</i> genes in the B-genome contributed to tissue development and fruit ripening.</p><p>Ripening of FJ was more rapid than BX during post-harvest ripening. BX required 8 and 14 DPH to reach the more-green-than-yellow and full-yellow stages, respectively, whereas FJ required 3 and 6 DPH to reach these stages, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">20</a>). The dominant expression of ethylene biosynthesis and fruit ripening-related gene pairs and the expansion of the ACO family in the B-genome could have contributed to increased ethylene production and faster fruit ripening in FJ compared to BX.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">The active starch metabolic pathway in the B-genome during fruit development and the ripening process</h3><p>Starch is the most widespread and abundant storage carbohydrate in plants. It is also a major component of cultivated banana, accumulating to high levels (~60–75% of dry weight) and leading to the presence of large starch granules (~8–30 m) during banana fruit development, along with near-complete conversion to soluble sugars during post-harvest ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hubbard, N. L., Pharr, D. M. &amp; Huber, S. C. Role of sucrose phosphate synthase in sucrose biosynthesis in ripening bananas and its relationship to the respiratory climacteric. Plant Physiol. 94, 201–208 (1990)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2060">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="do Nascimento, J. R. O. et al. Beta-amylase expression and starch degradation during banana ripening. Postharvest Biol. Tec. 40, 41–47 (2006)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2060_1">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tribess, T. et al. Thermal properties and resistant starch content of green banana flour (Musa cavendishii) produced at different drying conditions. LWT-Food Sci. Technol. 42, 1022–1025 (2009)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2060_2">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Jourda, C. et al. Lineage-specific evolutionary histories and regulation of major starch metabolism genes during banana ripening. Front. Plant. Sci. 7, 1778 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2063">51</a></sup>. Thus, banana could serve as an excellent model for the investigatation of starch metabolism in fresh starchy fruits. The major enzymes that are responsible for starch biosynthesis (sugars will eventually be exported transporter: SWEET; sucrose transporter: SUT; sucrose synthase: SuSy; UDP-glucose pyrophosphorylase: UGPase; ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase: AGPase; granule-bound starch synthase: GBSS; soluble starch synthase: SSS; starch branching enzyme: SBE; and starch debranching enzyme: DBE) and degradation (α-amylase: AMY; β-amylase: BMY; and starch phosphorylase: DPE) are encoded by multigenic families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jourda, C. et al. Lineage-specific evolutionary histories and regulation of major starch metabolism genes during banana ripening. Front. Plant. Sci. 7, 1778 (2016)." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2067">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Martin, C. &amp; Smith, A. M. Starch biosynthesis. Plant Cell 73, 2141–2145 (1995)." href="#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2067_1">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tiessen, A. et al. Starch synthesis in potato tubers is regulated by post-translation redox modification of ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase: a novel regulatory mechamism linking starch synthesis to the sucrose supply. Plant Cell 14, 2191–2213 (2002)." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2067_2">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tetlow, I. J. et al. Analysis of protein complexes in wheat amyloplasts reveals functional interactions among starch biosynthetic enzymes. Plant Physiol. 146, 1878–1891 (2008)." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2067_3">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xiao, Y. Y. et al. A comprehensive investigation of starch degradation process and identification of a transcriptional activator MabHLH6 during banana fruit ripening. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 151–164 (2017)." href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2067_4">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Miao, H. et al. Soluble starch synthase III-1 in amylopectin metabolism of banana fruit: characterization, expression, enzyme activity, and functional analyses. Front. Plant. Sci. 8, 454 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2070">56</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4a</a>). We identified 101 starch metabolism-related genes in the A-genome, including 77 in the starch synthesis pathway and 24 in the starch degradation pathway.
