Ninety-six such genes were identified in the B-genome, including 68 in the starch synthesis pathway and 28 in the starch degradation pathway (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">39</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Comparison of genomic expansion, evolutionary history and differential expression patterns of the starch metabolic pathway between M. acuminata (A-genome) and M. balbisiana (B-genome)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Comparison of genomic expansion, evolutionary history and differential expression patterns of the starch metabolic pathway between <i>M. acuminata</i> (A-genome) and <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B-genome).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0452-6/MediaObjects/41477_2019_452_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="856" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Overview of the starch biosynthesis and degradation pathway. <b>b</b>, Gene families in the starch metabolic pathway that are expanded in <i>M. acuminata</i> and <i>M. balbisiana</i>. <b>c</b>, Expression patterns of families AMY, BMY and DPE in the starch degradation pathway in BX, the A-subgenome of FJ and the B-subgenome of FJ during fruit-ripening stages. Horizontally oriented genes in the heat map indicate homoeologue gene pairs between the A- and B-genomes. White boxes with diagonals indicate that no homoeologue gene pairs were identified between the A- and B-genomes. Asterisks indicate expression dominance of homoeologue gene pairs between the A-subgenome of FJ and the B-subgenome of FJ. <b>d</b>,<b>e</b>, Synteny analyses of AMYs (<b>d</b>) and BMYs (<b>e</b>) between the A- and B-genomes. Red lines indicate paralogous gene pairs resulting from segmental/WGD-driven duplication, blue lines indicate homoeologous gene pairs, purple lines indicate tandem duplication, light blue strips indicate aligned syntenic blocks, light green strip indicates translocation block and light red strips indicate inversion blocks. The blocks in the outer ring represent location and length of genes; blue blocks represent genes from A-genome and orange blocks represent genes from B-genome. <b>f</b>, Starch contents at different stages of fruit development and ripening in BX and FJ. Error bars show standard error of the mean from three independent experiments (<i>n</i> = 3). <b>g</b>, Scanning electron microscopy of starch granules at different stages of fruit development and ripening in BX and FJ. The experiment was repeated three times independently with similar results.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In the starch synthesis pathway, five (SuSy, GBSS, SSS, SBE and DBE) out of nine families showed obvious expansion in the A- and B-genomes of banana, compared to seven other plant species that included two fruit plants, tomato and grape. These expansions suggest a potential significance in banana (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">40</a>). We characterized 54 homoeologue gene pairs from these families in the A- and B-genomes. Of these, 27 homoeologue gene pairs had expression dominance in root, leaf and fruit tissues with seven dominant in the A-subgenome and 20 in the B-subgenome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">21</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">41</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">45</a>). Consequently, the starch synthesis pathway is more active in different tissues within the B-subgenome than in the A-subgenome. Of those gene pairs with dominant expression in the B-subgenome, <i>MbSWEET17</i>, <i>MbSuSy1</i>, <i>MbSuSy2</i> and <i>MbSuSy9</i> had high expression levels (log<sub>2</sub>-based RPKM &gt; 6) at 0 DAF and 20 DAF, suggesting an important role in starch synthesis during fruit development (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">21</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">43</a>). In contrast, most of the starch synthesis-related genes that were unique to the A- or B-genome exhibited low expression levels (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">22</a>).</p><p>Within the starch degradation pathway, genome annotation indicated that families AMY and BMY had an obvious expansion in the banana A- and B-genomes compared to seven other plant species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">40</a>). We characterized 21 homoeologue gene pairs within this pathway from the A- and B-genomes. Among these gene pairs, 11 had dominant expression and were associated with the B-subgenome during fruit ripening (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4c</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">46</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">50</a>). Among the dominant genes, <i>MbAMY-2, MbAMY-3</i>, <i>MbAMY-8</i>, <i>MbBMY-6</i>, <i>MbBMY-8</i> and <i>MbDPE-2</i> exhibited high expression (log<sub>2</sub>-based RPKM &gt; 6 in at least one stage) during fruit ripening (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">48</a>). <i>MbAMY-1</i>, <i>-2</i>, <i>-3</i>, <i>-4</i>, <i>-5</i> and <i>MbAMY-6</i>, <i>-7</i>, <i>-8</i> showed close proximity and an evolutionary relationship, suggesting that these genes duplicated from a tandem copy (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">23</a>). <i>MbBMY-5</i>, <i>-8</i> and <i>MbBMY-6</i>, <i>-12</i> are two paralogous pairs in syntenic blocks of the B-genome and showed close evolutionary relationships, suggesting that these genes duplicated from WGD (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4e</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">24</a>). Taken together, these results suggest that tandem-driven <i>AMY</i> duplication and WGD-driven <i>BMY</i> duplication in the B-genome contribute to starch degradation.</p><p>The dominant expression of genes involved in starch biosynthesis in the B-subgenome could have led to increased starch accumulation during fruit development in FJ relative to BX (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4f,g</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">21</a>). In addition, the dominant expression of genes related to starch degradation in the B-subgenome also probably led to elevated starch degradation in FJ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Fig4">4c,f,g</a>). Therefore, the active starch metabolic pathway in the B-genome leads to marked starch accumulation and degradation during the fruit development and ripening processes, respectively.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Most cultivated bananas are triploid, having evolved from two wild diploid species, <i>M. acuminata</i> and <i>M. balbisiana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Simmonds, N. W. &amp; Shepherd, K. The taxonomy and origins of the cultivated bananas. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 55, 302–312 (1955)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2312">6</a></sup>. Our previous <i>M. acuminata</i> genome sequencing efforts provided genomic resources to inform banana breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2319">1</a></sup>. However, there is also an urgent need to develop the genomic resources of <i>M. balbisiana</i>, which is a crucial contributor to cultivated varieties. Improved understanding of the B-genome structure, subgenome evolution, homoeologous exchange, genetic diversity in polyploidy bananas and gene expansion and expression patterns will help in the design and application of breeding strategies for novel banana cultivars with improved traits.</p><p>Cycles of WGD followed by diploidization events have occurred across land plants, and have significantly contributed to their evolutionary success<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bennett, R. N. &amp; Wallsgrove, R. M. Secondary metabolites in plant defence mechanisms. New Phytol. 127, 617–633 (1994)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2329">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Baurens, F. C. et al. Recombination and large structural variations shape interspecific edible bananas genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 36, 97–111 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2332">25</a></sup>. Our results indicate that the B-genome may be more sensitive to fractionation than the A-genome after WGD, although the A- and B-subgenomes have diverged very recently. Variation in genomic structure between the A- and B-genomes consists of chromosome rearrangements and gene loss during diploidization, which has resulted in the functional divergence of subgenomes in polyploidy bananas. This divergence is supported by differential enrichment of expanded/contracted gene families between the A- and B-genomes and the expression dominance of homoeologue genes from A- and B-subgenomes in triploids. Although homoeologue expression dominance has been identified in certain polyploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531–537 (2015)." href="#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2336">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2336_1">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Grover, C. E. et al. Homoeolog expression bias and expression level dominance in allopolyploids. New Phytol. 196, 966–971 (2012)." href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2336_2">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225–1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2339">60</a></sup>, the relationship between homoeologue expression dominance and functional divergence (especially in regard to ethylene biosynthesis/starch metabolism) of subgenomes in triploids remains to be elucidated. Thus, these results provide an important basis for the improvement of agriculturally important traits by focusing selection on transcriptionally dominant genes. It is worth noting that homoeologous exchanges may obscure the signal of expression dominance in subgenomes of allopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Bird, K. A. et al. The causes and consequences of subgenome dominance in hybrids and recent polyploids. New Phytol. 220, 87–93 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2343">61</a></sup>. The extensive homoeologue exchanges in allopolyploid bananas may remove many progenitor genome conflicts that result in subgenome biases in gene content and expression. Thus, homoeologous exchanges may contribute to the diversity of homoeologue expression dominance and induce a series of rapid genetic and epigenetic modifications for agronomic traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Bird, K. A. et al. The causes and consequences of subgenome dominance in hybrids and recent polyploids. New Phytol. 220, 87–93 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2347">61</a></sup>.</p><p>Previous studies have suggested an important role of ethylene production in fruit ripening and starch metabolism with regard to fruit quality post-harvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jourda, C. et al. Expansion of banana (Musa acuminata) gene families involved in ethylene biosynthesis and signalling after lineage specific whole genome duplications. New Phytol. 202, 986–1000 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2354">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Liu, X. et al. Characterization of ethylene biosynthesis associated with ripening in banana fruit. Plant Physiol. 121, 1257–1265 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2357">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Xiao, Y. Y. et al. A comprehensive investigation of starch degradation process and identification of a transcriptional activator MabHLH6 during banana fruit ripening. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 151–164 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2360">55</a></sup>. However, the genetic mechanisms underlying polyploid fruit ripening are less well known. Here, we analysed biological processes related to these two pathways in triploid bananas. We identified significant genomic expansions and dominant expression of homoeologue genes in the B-genome at the pathway level of gene families and, most notably, in the ACO family, known to play a critical role in ethylene production. Our analysis revealed the origin and evolution of crucial gene families in these pathways, particularly for the independent origin of the <i>MaACO2</i>/<i>MbACO6</i> and <i>MaACO3</i>/<i>MbACO7</i> gene pairs and their specific function in fruit ripening. Moreover, we identified that this tandem event has led to expansion of <i>ACO</i> genes in the B-genome and to strong expression of these genes during fruit ripening. Our analysis also indicated a potential link between the dominant expression of homoeologue genes and the expansion of gene families with fruit ripening and starch metabolism. Previous studies have demonstrated that the B-genome is associated with improved vigour and tolerance to both biotic and abiotic stresses. Consequently, the B-genome is a target for banana breeding programmes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davey, M. W. et al. A draft Musa balbisiana genome sequence for molecular genetics in polyploid, inter-and intra-specific Musa hybrids. BMC Genom. 14, 683 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2380">2</a></sup>. Here, we highlight that the B-genome is of great importance in ethylene biosynthesis and post-harvest banana ripening, which will further our understanding of ripening mechanisms in polyploid fruit.</p><p>The <i>M. balbisiana</i> genome assembly, along with our previously acquired <i>M. acuminata</i> genome data, may aid in the discovery of cultivar-specific sequences that are related to important cultivar-specific traits, including shelf life, quality and stress tolerance. Thus, these resources can be used to build molecular characterization strategies for various cultivars to assist in rapid quality control and the conservation of biodiversity. The data from this study also pave the way for whole-genome association studies, germplasm improvement and genetic modification of bananas to meet increasing commercial demands. These genomic resources and results also reinforce the use of the banana as an appropriate model to study subgenome evolution and fruit biology in triploid variants. Due to the sterility and seedlessness of banana cultivars, further efforts will be needed to leverage the key gene resources from precisely characterized germplasms to achieve effective breeding schemes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Sample collection</h3><p>A double haploid of the wild diploid genotype PKW; 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 22 was provided by the Centre de Coopération Internationale en Recherche Agronomique pour le Développement (CIRAD) for genome sequencing. Fresh, unexpanded leaves were harvested and then frozen immediately with liquid nitrogen to preserve genomic DNA for isolation. High-molecular weight genomic DNA was extracted using a standard cetyltrimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Murray, M. G. &amp; Thompson, W. F. Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 8, 4321–4326 (1980)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2411">62</a></sup>. DNA integrity was assessed by agarose gel electrophoresis (concentration of agarose gel, 1%; voltage, 150 V; electrophoresis time, 40 min). Finally, DNA was purified from the gel using a QIAquick Gel Extraction kit (QIAGEN).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>One paired-end and eight mate-pair libraries were constructed for short-read sequencing on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform, which generated around 86.34 Gb (166× coverage) of high-quality data. For long-read DNA sequencing, 5.79 million SMRT long reads (58.99 Gb data, 113× coverage) were sequenced using the PacBio Sequel system with libraries of 20-kb insert size; sub-reads had a mean length of 10.2 kb and N50 length of 16.6 kb. One Hi-C library was prepared and sequenced on Illumina NovaSeq 6000 to generate 71.96 Gb (138× coverage) of high-quality data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2423">63</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">1</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#Sec28">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly</h3><p>De novo assembly of DH-PKW was performed using wtdbg (v.1.2.8; <a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg">https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg</a>) based on PacBio data (only reads longer than 1 kb were used in assembly). The assembled genome was corrected for two rounds using the ‘wtdbg-cns’ programme in the wtdbg package. We then used the algorithm Arrow (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>), which takes into account all of the underlying data and the raw quality values inherent in SMRT sequencing, to polish the assembly again for the final consensus accuracies. The final consensus contigs were scaffolded using the SSPACE-standard programme<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Boetzer, M. et al. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2455">64</a></sup> (v.3.0) with meta-pair reads from libraries of insert size 2–20 kb. Based on Hi-C data, 430.02-Mb scaffolds were anchored to 11 pseudo-molecules using LACHESIS software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2459">13</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">2</a>). Chromosomes were numbered according to the linkage group nomenclature adopted for <i>M. acuminata</i>. Additional details regarding genome assembly are provided in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Evaluation of assembly quality</h3><p>BUSCO (v.3) was used to assess assembly completeness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2481">11</a></sup>. We mapped 29,610 <i>M. acuminata</i>-expressed sequence tags (ESTs) to the assembled genome using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2488">65</a></sup> (v.35) with default parameters. In total, 93.59% of the ESTs were aligned to the genome with identity &gt;90%. Additionally, BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2492">66</a></sup> v.0.7.12 (aln -l 35) was used to map 59× Illumina reads to the assembly, and 96.11% of the reads were mapped to the assembled genome. Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome annotation</h3><p>Repetitive sequences within the <i>M. balbisiana</i> genome were identified by a combination of homology-based and de novo approaches (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">1</a>). Gene structures were annotated iteratively using three main approaches (ab initio predictions, homologue proteins and transcriptome data) that were combined using MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. Genome annotation and curation using MAKER and MAKER‐P. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 48, 4–11 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2514">15</a></sup> (v.2.31.8) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">5</a>). Gene functions were annotated according to the best match of the alignments using blastp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Mount, D. W. Using the basic local alignment search tool (BLAST). Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/pdb.top17&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2521">67</a></sup> (<i>E</i>-value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>–5</sup>) against the Swiss-Prot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2531">68</a></sup>, TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2535">68</a></sup>, NR (<a href="https://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/blast/db/FASTA/">https://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/blast/db/FASTA/</a>), KOG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinformatics 4, 41 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2546">69</a></sup> and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2551">70</a></sup> databases. Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Transcription factors</h3><p>We used the iTAK programme<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zheng, Y. et al. iTAK: a program for genome-wide prediction and classification of plant transcription factors, transcriptional regulators, and protein kinases. Mol. Plant 9, 1667–1670 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2566">16</a></sup> to identify transcription factors based on the consensus rules that are mainly summarized within PlnTFDB and PlantTFDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Pérez-Rodríguez, P. et al. PlnTFDB: updated content and new features of the plant transcription factor database. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D822–D827 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2570">71</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Jin, J. et al. PlantTFDB 3.0: a portal for the functional and evolutionary study of plant transcription factors. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1182–D1187 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2573">72</a></sup>, with families from PlantTFcat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Dai, X. et al. PlantTFcat: an online plant transcription factor and transcriptional regulator categorization and analysis tool. BMC Bioinformatics 14, 321 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2577">73</a></sup> and AtTFDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Davuluri, R. V. et al. AGRIS: Arabidopsis gene regulatory information server, an information resource of Arabidopsis cis-regulatory elements and transcription factors. BMC Bioinformatics 4, 25 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2581">74</a></sup> used as supporting evidence. In total, we identified 3,329 transcription factor genes in <i>M. balbisiana</i> and 3,899 in <i>M. acuminata</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene family analysis</h3><p>A total of 500,142 genes from 16 plant species with available whole-genome sequences were used for gene family clustering analysis. BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Mount, D. W. Using the basic local alignment search tool (BLAST). Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/pdb.top17&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2603">67</a></sup> (v.2.2.26; -p blastp) was used to generate pairwise protein sequence alignments with <i>E</i>-values of &lt; 1 × 10<sup>–5</sup>. Then, OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2612">22</a></sup> was used to cluster similar genes by setting the main inflation value at 1.5 and using default settings for the other parameters. These analyses resulted in 39,358 gene families comprising 393,700 genes from the 16 species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">7</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><p>We identified 519 single-copy gene families shared among the 16 species, and constructed a phylogenetic tree using MrBayes (v.3.1.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2626">75</a></sup> software with the general time-reversible model (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">4</a>). Divergence times for the 16 species were also estimated based on fourfold degenerate sites of all single-copy orthologous genes using the MCMCTree programme in the PAML package (v.4.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2633">76</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>CAFÉ (v.2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="De Bie, T. et al. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2643">23</a></sup> was used to analyse the expansion and contraction of gene families. A random birth-and-death model was used to assess gene gain or loss in gene families across the specified phylogenetic tree (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">9</a>). Families with <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05 were considered as significant expansion or contraction, and pathway enrichment analysis of these families was conducted using the enrichment pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Huang, D. W., Sherman, B. T. &amp; Lempicki, R. A. Bioinformatics enrichment tools: paths toward the comprehensive functional analysis of large gene lists. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 1–13 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2653">77</a></sup>. Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Whole-genome alignment analysis</h3><p>MCSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2668">78</a></sup> (parameters: -a -e 1e-5 -s 5) was used to detect collinearity within <i>M. acuminata</i> (A-genome) and <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B-genome) and among various species. Syntenic blocks containing at least ten gene pairs were obtained. All of the orthologous and paralogous gene pairs were extracted from the syntenic blocks for calculation of 4dTv<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2678">79</a></sup> distances using the HKY substitution model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Hasegawa, M., Kishino, H. &amp; Yano, T. A. Dating of the human-ape splitting by a molecular clock of mitochondrial DNA. J. Mol. Evol. 22, 160–174 (1985)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2682">80</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">8</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Orthologous gene pair analysis</h3><p>BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Mount, D. W. Using the basic local alignment search tool (BLAST). Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/pdb.top17&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2701">67</a></sup> (v.2.2.26, -p blastp) was used to align <i>M. acuminata</i> proteins to <i>M. balbisiana</i> proteins for identification of orthologous genes. The value 1 × 10<sup>–5</sup> was used as a cut-off to define the raw orthologues. We then filtered the BLAST results using two parameters (CIP ≥ 60% and CALP ≥ 60% (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Salse, J. et al. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief. Bioinformatics 10, 619–630 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2713">35</a></sup>). We identified 25,717 orthologous gene pairs (81.83% consistency with syntenic blocks) between <i>M. acuminata</i> and <i>M. balbisiana</i> using these two parameters (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">22</a>). The orthologous gene pairs were first aligned using MUSCLE (v.3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2727">81</a></sup>, then the Ka/Ks ratio of each gene pair was calculated using KaKs_Calculator (v.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wang, D. et al. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genom. Proteom. Bioinf. 8, 77–80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2731">82</a></sup> with model yn00. The significant difference between Ka/Ks values was detected by Student’s <i>t</i>-test.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Re-sequencing analysis</h3><p>Nine different genotypes of banana were used for re-sequencing, including the triploid plants BaXiJiao (subgroup <i>Cavendish</i>, AAA), Gros_Michel (subgroup <i>Gros Michel</i>, AAA), FenJiao (subgroup Pisang Awak, ABB), Kamaramasenge (AAB) and Pelipita (ABB), in addition to the diploid plants Pisang_Mas (subgroup Sucrier, AA), Pisang_Kra (subsp. <i>malaccensis</i>, AA), DH-PKW (BB) and balbisiana (BB) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">13</a>). BaXiJiao, Gros_Michel and FenJiao were obtained from the Tissue Culture Centre of the Chinese Academy of Tropical Agricultural Sciences (CATAS). Pelipita, Pisang_Mas, Pisang_Kra, <i>M. balbisiana</i> and Kamaramasenge were provided by the Bioversity International Musa Transit Centre. Genomic DNA was extracted from fresh leaves of seedlings at the five-leaf stage using the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Murray, M. G. &amp; Thompson, W. F. Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 8, 4321–4326 (1980)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2762">62</a></sup>.</p><p>Paired-end reads with libraries of 500-bp insert size were aligned to the A- and B-genomes simultaneously using BWA (v.0.7.12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2769">66</a></sup> with the parameters ‘bwa aln -t 20 -l 35’ (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">14</a>), and only uniquely mapped reads were kept. Potential PCR duplicates were marked using Picard (v.1.54, <a href="https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>) and indexed using the SAMtools package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2783">83</a></sup>. The Genome Analysis Toolkit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2787">84</a></sup> was then used to infer SNPs and InDels. SNP identifications were filtered based on the following parameters: ‘QD &lt; 2.0 || FS &gt; 60.0 || MQ &lt; 40.0 || MQRankSum &lt; −12.5 || ReadPosRankSum &lt; −8.0’, and InDels were filtered based on the following parameters: ‘QD &lt; 2.0 || FS &gt; 200.0 || ReadPosRankSum &lt; −20.0’ (Supplementary Tables 16 and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">17</a>). Breakdancer (<a href="http://breakdancer.sourceforge.net/">http://breakdancer.sourceforge.net/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Chen, K. et al. BreakDancer: an algorithm for high-resolution mapping of genomic structural variation. Nat. Methods 6, 677–681 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2802">85</a></sup> was used to detect structural variations by checking paired-end reads with an abnormal orientation and/or span. The final structural variations were filtered using the following requirements: (1) minimum size of 100 and maximum size of 1,000,000; (2) minimum score of ≥30; and (3) minimum number of required reads supporting each structural variation ≥5 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">18</a>). Nucleotide diversity (π) was analysed using VCFtools (v.0.1.13; <a href="https://vcftools.github.io/man_latest.html">https://vcftools.github.io/man_latest.html</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analysis of homoeologous exchanges</h3><p>Assessment of read coverage depth was used to detect homoeologous exchanges between the A- and B-subgenomes. We inferred these based on cases where reads coverage of a given region on one parental genome was significantly high while the orthologous one was low or had no coverage. High coverage indicates duplication, and low or no coverage indicates loss<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chalhoub, Boulos et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2825">34</a></sup>. The uniquely mapped paired-end reads were used to calculate the coverage depth of each sample on the A- and B-genomes (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">25</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">27</a>). According to coverage depth, we detected homoeologous exchanges in the triploids ‘FenJiao (ABB)’, ‘Pelipita (ABB)’ and ‘Kamaramasenge (AAB)’ (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">15</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Transcriptome analysis</h3><p>Banana fruits at different stages of development (0, 20 and 80 DAF) and ripening (8 and 14 DPH for BX, 3 and 6 DPH for FJ) were obtained from the banana plantation at the Institute of Tropical Bioscience and Biotechnology (Chengmai, Hainan, 20° N, 110° E). Two-month-old BX and FJ banana seedlings were obtained from the Tissue Culture Centre of CATAS and cultured in Hoagland’s solution. Seedlings at the five-leaf stage were treated with 200 mM mannitol for 7 days, 300 mM NaCl for 7 days and low-temperature conditions (4 °C) for 22 h. Fruit, root and leaf samples were frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at −80 °C until RNA extraction for transcriptome analysis.</p><p>Total RNAs were isolated using a plant RNA extraction kit (TIANGEN). Total RNA (3 μg) from each sample was converted to complementary DNA using a RevertAid First-Strand cDNA Synthesis Kit (Fermentas). cDNA libraries were constructed using TruSeq RNA Library Preparation Kit v.2, and were subsequently sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform using the Illumina RNA-Seq protocol. Two biological replicates were used for each sample. A total of 159.14 Gb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">21</a>) of high-quality clean data were produced. Gene expression levels were calculated as RPKM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Mortazavi, A. et al. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621–628 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2855">86</a></sup>. Differentially expressed genes were identified by methods previously established with the read count of two replicates for each gene (fold change ≥2; false discovery rate ≤0.001)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Audic, S. &amp; Claverie, J. M. The significance of digital gene expression profiles. Genome Res. 7, 986–995 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2859">87</a></sup>. Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM1">Supplementary note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Weighted gene co-expression network analysis</h3><p>Gene expression patterns for all identified genes were used to construct a co-expression network using WGCNA (v.1.47)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Langfelder, P. &amp; Horvath, S. WGCNA: an R package for weighted correlation network analysis. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 559 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2874">39</a></sup>. Genes without expression detected in all tissues were removed before analyses. Soft thresholds were set based on the scale-free topology criterion employed in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Zhang, B. &amp; Horvath, S. A general framework for weighted gene co-expression network analysis. Stat. Appl. Genet. Mol. Biol. 4, 17 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2878">88</a></sup>. An adjacency matrix was developed using squared Euclidean distance values, and the topological overlap matrix was calculated for unsigned network detection using the Pearson method. Co-expression coefficients &gt;0.55 between the target genes were then selected. Finally, the network connections were visualized using cytoscape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Shannon, P. et al. Cytoscape: a software environment for integrated models of biomolecular interaction networks. Genome Res. 13, 2498–2504 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2882">89</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Identification of gene families involved in ethylene biosynthesis and starch metabolism pathways</h3><p>We compared genes related to ethylene biosynthesis and starch metabolism in the <i>M. balbisiana</i> genome to those annotated in both <i>M. acuminata</i> and other plant genomes, including <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>. We retrieved protein sequences of these gene families from 16 species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM3">51</a>) for homologue-based searches with the criteria similarity &gt;80% and coverage &gt;80%. We then confirmed the presence of the conserved domain within all protein sequences and removed members without a complete domain.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Determination of total starch content</h3><p>Banana pulp was immersed in 0.5% sodium bisulfite for 10 min to prevent browning, and then dried at 40 °C for 24 h. Pulp was then ground and centrifuged. The residue was suspended in 5 ml of 80% Ca(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> in a boiling water bath for 10 min then centrifuged for 4 min at 4,000 r.p.m. The supernatant was transferred to a 20-ml volumetric flask and the residue was extracted twice with 80% Ca(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, which yielded a combined extract volume of 20 ml. All experiments were repeated three times. The total starch content was assessed following methods described by Yang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Yang, J. &amp; Bi, C. A half-grain method for analyzing the amylose content in rice grains and its application. Acta Agron. Sin. 18, 366–372 (1992)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d64575e2941">90</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Scanning electron microscopy</h3><p>Samples were fixed in stubs using double-faced tape and coated with a 10-nm platinum layer in a Bal-tec MEDo020 coating system (Kettleshulme). The prepared samples were analysed using an FEI Quanta 600 FEG scanning electron microscope (FEI Co.). Observations were performed in secondary electron mode while operating at 15 kV.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Measurement of ethylene production during fruit post-harvest stage</h3><p>Ethylene production was measured by enclosing fruit samples in an airtight container for 2 h at 25 °C. After incubation, 1 ml of the headspace gas was withdrawn and injected into a gas chromatograph (GC-2010, Shimadzu) fitted with a flame ionization detector and an activated alumina column. Ethylene production measurements were obtained as recommended by the manufacturer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0452-6#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Raw sequence reads for B-genome assembly and transcriptome for all samples were deposited in the CNSA (<a href="https://db.cngb.org/search/project/CNP0000292/">https://db.cngb.org/search/project/CNP0000292/</a>) of CNGBdb with accession number CNP0000292 and Sequence Read Archive of the National Centre for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) under the BioProject (No. PRJNA432894). Genome assembly and annotation of DH-PKW were submitted to NCBI (No. PYDT00000000). Assembly and gene annotation of the A-genome (DH-Pahang) are available on the Banana Genome Hub (<a href="http://banana-genome-hub.southgreen.fr/">http://banana-genome-hub.southgreen.fr/</a>).</p>
